⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا لنفسه، وهذا مما لا شك فيه لمن عقل وأنصف من نفسه. والذي يؤيد هذا أن الجبلة البشرية مهيأة لقبول الحكم والعلوم معدة للزيادة وبلوغ درجة الكمال فيها عند إفادة الحكيم المرشد إياها، إذ هى ليست ممن لا يجوز عليه الجهل بالأشياء، ولا مما يمتنع عليه قبول العلوم والحكم مع تفنن أقسامها وتكثر أعدادها . ثم لا شك أن صانع العالم حكيم لا يسفه عليم لا يجهل، ومن هذا وصفه، فليس بممتنع منه إمداد المجبول على النقيصة بما يوجب زوالها عنه ويورث له الكمال وبلوغ الدرجة العالية في العلم والحكمة، بل هو من الممكن الذي جرت به العادة من الموسومين بالحكمة المشهورين بالتحلي بالعلوم إذ لم يخل عصر من الأعصار ممن يقوم من العلماء لإفادة ما سعد به من العلم المحتاجين إليه العارين عنه ليبلغوا ببركته الكمال وتندفع عنهم نقيصة الجهل بذلك. فالله تعالى مع كمال حكمته ورحمته على عبادة أولى أن لا يمتنع منه ومما يدل على صحة إرسال الله تعالى الرسل وجوازه هو أنه إذا لم يكن في إرسالهم إفساد للتكليف ولا إبطال للمنحة ولا إيجاب قلب بعض الأدلة ولا إخراج للقديم عن قدمه ولا قلب لبعض الحقائق ولا إلحاق نقيصة بالمرسل جل ذكره وكان في الرسالة تعريض الخلق من المكلفين لثواب جزيل ونفع عظيم صح ذلك في حكمته وكان عدلا فعله سبحانه.. ثم إن اليهود والنصارى والمسلمين قد أطبقوا على نقل أعلام موسى وعيسى ومحمد عليهم السلام، وأن الكذب يستحيل جوازه على مثلهم من ناحية التراسل والتكاتب والمواطأة على ذلك، لأن تمام ذلك وانتظامه من مثلهم محال متعذر في العادة
Not for itself, and this is not doubtful to one who reasons and is fair to himself. And what supports this is that human nature is prepared to accept rulings and sciences, ready for increase and reaching the degree of perfection in them when imparted by the wise guide, for it is not one upon which ignorance of things is impossible, nor one which cannot accept sciences and rulings despite the variety of their kinds and multiplicity of their numbers. Then there is no doubt that the Maker of the world is wise, not foolish, knowing, not ignorant. And He is described thus, so it is not impossible for Him to supply the one created with deficiency with that which necessitates its removal from him and gives him perfection and reaching the high degree in knowledge and wisdom. Rather, it is among the possible things that are customary for those characterized by wisdom and famous for adorning themselves with sciences, for no age has been devoid of those among scholars who impart the knowledge they have been blessed with to those who need it and are deprived of it, so that through his blessing they may attain perfection and the deficiency of ignorance may be repelled from them. God Almighty, with the perfection of His wisdom and mercy upon His servants, is more worthy that it should not be impossible for Him. And among what indicates the validity and permissibility of God's sending messengers is that if in their sending there is no corruption of obligation, no nullification of favor, no necessitating of altering some evidences, no removal of the Eternal from His eternality, no alteration of some realities, and no attaching of deficiency to the Sender (glorious is His mention), and in the message there is exposure of the obligated creatures to abundant reward and great benefit, then that is valid in His wisdom and His action is just. Then the Jews, Christians, and Muslims have all agreed on the transmission of the signs of Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad (peace be upon them), and that lying is impossible for the likes of them in terms of correspondence, writing, and collusion on that, because the completion and organization of that from the likes of them is impossible and unattainable by custom.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the validity and wisdom of God sending messengers. The author begins by stating that it is undeniable for any rational and fair person that human nature is inherently prepared to receive rulings and sciences. This nature is not incapable of acquiring knowledge; rather, it is designed for growth and perfection when a wise guide imparts it. The author then argues that the Creator of the world is inherently wise and knowledgeable, not foolish or ignorant. Therefore, it is not impossible for Him to provide guidance to humans, who are created with deficiency, in order to remove that deficiency and bring them to perfection in knowledge and wisdom. In fact, this is consistent with the customary practice of wise people in every age: scholars teach others who need knowledge. Hence, God, with His perfect wisdom and mercy, is even more worthy of not withholding such guidance. The author then provides an additional proof: sending messengers is valid if it does not corrupt divine obligation, nullify favor, alter evidences, affect God's eternity, change realities, or attach deficiency to God. Since it leads to great reward for the obligated, it is just and wise. Finally, he notes the consensus of Jews, Christians, and Muslims on the transmission of miracles from Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad, and that lying is impossible for such prophets due to the impossibility of collusion across time and distance.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's self-sufficiency prevents Him from caring about human perfection.
Correction: The passage shows that God's wisdom and mercy necessitate providing guidance, just as wise people in every age teach others. Far from being prevented, it is consistent with His attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it not impossible for God to send messengers?

B) Because God is wise and merciful, and it is consistent with His attributes to provide means for human perfection.
C) Because scholars in every age teach others.
D) Because the prophets have proven their truthfulness through miracles.

Key Takeaway
God, in His wisdom and mercy, sends messengers to guide humanity to perfection, and this is supported by rational argument and historical consensus.

The passage argues that God's wisdom necessitates sending messengers to remove ignorance. If you truly believe that God is wise and merciful, do you treat the coming of the Prophet (peace be upon him) as the greatest gift for your own perfection? Honestly, how much time do you spend learning from the Quran and Sunnah compared to worldly pursuits? What needs to change today?
?
You believe in God's justice and mercy. But does your life reflect gratitude for the means of guidance He provided? Reflect on a specific teaching of the Prophet (peace be upon him) that you have neglected. Write down a commitment to act on it this week, and hold yourself accountable.

Prophethood

The Objection of Foolishness and the Author's First Counter
The author refutes the initial objection that divine obligation is foolishness by questioning the analogy between worldly and divine actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial objection that divine obligation is foolishness and the author's first counter-argument that questions the universal validity of the analogy between the witnessed (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib).

Core Idea
The analogy between worldly and divine actions fails because God's nature is fundamentally different.

The opponents claim that commanding without benefit is foolishness, citing worldly examples. The author responds by asking them to prove that every worldly foolish act is also foolish in the unseen, and gives a counterexample: God created the world without benefiting Himself, yet He is not foolish.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening lines where the opponents argue that divine obligation is foolishness because it commands without benefit to the commander.
Notice the key term 'safah' (foolishness).
2
Follow the author's counter-question
The author asks: Is everything that is foolish in the witnessed world also foolish in the unseen? And is something foolish from one agent also foolish from another? This challenges the universal application of the analogy.
3
Examine the counterexample
The author points out that in the witnessed, a person who does an act with no benefit is foolish, yet God created the world without benefit to Himself, but He is not foolish. This shows the analogy is not valid.
Why can't God benefit? Because benefit implies need, which is a deficiency, and God is free from deficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على شبه الخصوم وأما ما زعموا أن ورود التكليف منه سفه لما فيه من أمر بما لا منفعة فيه للآمر، ونهي عما لا مضرة في وجوده للناهي وهذا سفه. قيل لهم: لم قلتم: إن هذا سفه؟ فإن قالوا: هذا في الشاهد سفه فكذا يكون في الغائب سفها. قيل: أكل ما كان في الشاهد من فاعل سفها، كان في الغائب سفها؟ وكذا ما كان من فاعل سفها أكان من غيره سفها؟ فإن قالوا: لا أبطلوا دليلهم، وإن قالوا: نعم، طولبوا بالدليل. ثم قيل لهم: أليس أن في الشاهد من فعل فعلاً لا منفعة له فيه فهو سفيه، والله تعالى أنشأ العالم وخلقه لا لمنفعة إذ يستحيل عليه الانتفاع لما هو دليل الحاجة وهي نقيصة والنقيصة من أمارات الحدث، ومع ذلك لم يكن سفيها، دل أن التسوية بين الشاهد والغائب غير ثابتة. فإن قالوا في الشاهد إنما يكون الفاعل لا لمنفعة نفسه سفيها إذ كان غيره لا ينتفع أيضا به، فأما إذا كان ينتفع به غيره فليس بسفيه. الله تعالى فعل ما فعل لينتفع به غيره، وهذا ليس بسفه في الشاهد فلم يكن في الغائب سفها. قيل: إن الله تعالى خلق أشياء لا ينتفع بها أحد البتة كالأجزاء الداخلة في تخوم الأرض والجبال الشاهقة، فإن أحدًا من الخلائق لا يطلع على تلك الأجزاء ولا يشاهدها لاستحالة دخول شيء من الأشياء في الأجسام المصمتة ليعاين أجزاءها لم يكن الله تعالى بذلك سفيها. ومن فعل ما لا ينتفع هو به ولا غيره في الشاهد سفيه، فلما افترق الحال بين الشاهد والغائب في الفصل يفترق أيضا في الأمر. ثم نقول: دعواكم أن في الشاهد من يفعل فعلاً لا ينتفع هو به لم يكن سفها ومع ذلك إذ كان ينتفع به غيره قلنا: ليس كذلك بل يعد سفها. فلم قلتم ذلك. فإن قالوا: من أطعم جائعًا أو كسا عاريًا لا يعد سفيها وإن كان لا ينتفع هو به، لما أن غيره ينتفع به وهو
Refutation of the doubts of the opponents: As for their claim that the occurrence of obligation from Him is foolishness because it involves commanding what has no benefit for the commander and forbidding what has no harm in its existence for the forbidded, and this is foolishness. It was said to them: Why did you say that this is foolishness? If they say: This in the witnessed is foolishness, so likewise it is foolishness in the unseen. It was said: Is everything that in the witnessed is foolishness from an agent necessarily foolishness in the unseen? And likewise, what is foolishness from an agent, would it be foolishness from another? If they say: No, they invalidate their evidence. If they say: Yes, they are asked for proof. Then it was said to them: Is it not that in the witnessed, one who does an act with no benefit in it is foolish, yet God Almighty originated the world and created it without benefit, since benefiting is impossible for Him because it indicates need, which is a deficiency, and deficiency is a sign of temporality, and despite that He was not foolish? This shows that the analogy between the witnessed and the unseen is not established. If they say: In the witnessed, an agent is only foolish if he acts with no benefit to himself while others also do not benefit from it; but if others benefit from it, he is not foolish. God Almighty did what He did so that others would benefit from it, and this is not foolishness in the witnessed, so it was not foolishness in the unseen. It was said: God Almighty created things from which no one benefits at all, like parts deep inside the earth and high mountains; indeed, no creature can see those parts or observe them due to the impossibility of anything entering solid bodies to see their parts, yet God was not foolish by that. And one who does something from which neither he nor others benefit is foolish in the witnessed. So when the case differs between the witnessed and the unseen in one aspect, it also differs in the matter. Then we say: Your claim that in the witnessed one who does an act without benefiting himself is not foolish—while that is when others benefit from it—we say: Not so, rather he is considered foolish. So why did you say that? If they say: One who feeds a hungry person or clothes a naked person is not considered foolish even if he does not benefit himself, because others benefit from it, and he
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that obligating creatures is foolishness (safah) because God commands what does not benefit Him and forbids what does not harm Him. The author engages in a dialectical argument. First, he asks the opponents why they consider it foolishness. They argue by analogy: in the witnessed world, such action is foolish, so it must be in the unseen. The author counters by questioning the universal applicability of this analogy: is every foolish action in the witnessed also foolish in the unseen? And does something foolish from one agent remain foolish from another? If they deny this, their analogy collapses; if they affirm, they must provide proof. The author then gives a counterexample: in the witnessed, a person who does an act with no benefit is foolish, yet God created the world without benefiting Himself, as benefit implies need which is a deficiency, yet God is not foolish. This shows the analogy is invalid. The opponents then modify: in the witnessed, an agent is only foolish if neither he nor others benefit; if others benefit, it is not foolish. God's actions benefit others, so it is not foolish. The author responds by pointing out that God created things like hidden parts of the earth and high mountains that no one can see or benefit from, yet God is not foolish. Finally, he challenges their initial premise: they claim that in the witnessed, one who does an act without benefiting himself is not foolish if others benefit—but the author counters that such a person is indeed considered foolish. The passage ends mid-argument with the opponents giving an example of feeding the hungry, which they claim is not foolish even if no personal benefit.

Key Terms
السفه
التكليف
الشاهد
الغائب
التسوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy between worldly and divine actions is always valid.
Correction: The author shows that because God's nature is different (no need), the analogy fails.

Checkpoint
What logical strategy does the author use to refute the claim that divine obligation is foolishness in this passage?
A) He argues that God benefits from creation.
B) He shows that the analogy between the witnessed and the unseen is flawed by giving a counterexample from God's creation without benefit.
C) He denies that God commands anything.
D) He claims that foolishness is only a human concept.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the objection by demonstrating that the analogy between worldly and divine actions is invalid because God's lack of need means His actions cannot be judged by worldly standards of foolishness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command from God that brings you no apparent benefit, do you trust that His wisdom surpasses your understanding, or do you secretly judge Him by human standards? What would it take for you to obey without seeing the benefit?
?
The author shows that God needs nothing from His creation. In what areas of your life are you seeking your own benefit rather than serving God purely? Will you commit to one act of worship this week that brings you no worldly gain?
?
The opponents measured God by their own limited reason. Where in your heart have you set up your own judgment over God's wisdom? Today, will you surrender that pride and accept that His ways are higher than yours?

The Opponents' Modified Argument and Further Refutation
The author continues refuting the objection by challenging the modified analogy and giving a counterexample of creations with no perceivable benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' modified argument that divine obligation benefits others and the author's counter-argument involving creations that benefit no one.

Core Idea
Even if God's actions benefit others, some creations benefit no one, proving the analogy inconsistent.

The opponents adjust their stance: an act is only foolish if neither the agent nor others benefit. Since God's actions benefit creatures, it's not foolish. The author points out that God created hidden parts of the earth and high mountains that no one can perceive, yet He is not foolish. Finally, he challenges their premise that benefiting others exempts from foolishness.

Guided Path
1
Read the modified objection
The opponents now claim that an act is only foolish if it benefits neither the agent nor anyone else. Since God's actions benefit creatures, it is not foolish.
2
Examine the author's counterexample
The author points out that God created things like deep earth and high mountains that no one can see or benefit from, yet God is not considered foolish. This shows the opponents' criterion is not consistently applied.
This challenges the idea that benefiting others is sufficient to avoid foolishness.
3
Follow the final challenge
The author then questions why they think an act that benefits others is not foolish in the witnessed, implying that it still could be considered foolish. The argument is cut off but the intent is clear.
The author is turning the tables on the opponents' own criterion.

Rejecting the Merely Possible View of Prophethood
A fragment rejecting a minimalist view of prophethood's necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's rejection of the position that sending messengers is merely possible and his insistence on its necessity.

Core Idea
Sending messengers is not merely possible; it is necessary.

In this passage, the author takes aim at those who limit prophethood to the realm of possibility without affirming its necessity. He begins a new argument to establish that sending messengers is not just an option but a rational and moral necessity. Even though the passage is incomplete, its direction is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting that the sentence breaks off. Understand that this is a fragment from a larger argument.
The passage is incomplete due to missing text in the source.
2
Identify the author's target
The author targets those who hold that sending messengers is merely possible (within the realm of possibilities) and do not rise to saying it is necessary.
This is a minimalist view of prophethood.
3
Understand key terms
الممكنات (al-mumkinat) refers to things that are merely possible; واجبة الوجود (wajibat al-wujud) refers to necessary existence. The author wants to elevate the status of prophethood from possible to necessary.
4
Reflect on the argument's direction
Even though the sentence is cut off, the author likely intends to argue that sending messengers is rationally or morally necessary, not just optional. The fragment establishes his stance against a minimalist view.
The full argument is not preserved, but the conclusion can be inferred.

The argument is cut off, but the direction is clear: the author intends to assert that sending messengers is not merely possible but morally or rationally necessary, a common position in Maturidi theology. Refuting the Denial of Prophet-Pretender Distinction
Nasafi refutes arguments against the prophet-pretender distinction by showing they imply God's incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why objections to the prophet-pretender distinction fail because they imply God's incapacity, which is impossible given His perfect power and will.

Core Idea
Denying God's ability to distinguish prophet from pretender denies His perfect power.

Nasafi now turns to rebut those who argue that no reliable distinction can be made between a true prophet and a false claimant. Their objections—whether based on the impossibility of changing natural laws or the possibility of faking miracles—ultimately suggest that God cannot tell truth from falsehood. This lesson unpacks why such reasoning is self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponents' arguments
Read the passage and find the two reasons given for why distinguishing a prophet from a pretender is supposedly impossible: (1) impossibility of changing natures, (2) possibility of opposition (i.e., faking miracles).
These are the 'other faction's' claims.
2
See the implication Nasafi draws
Notice that Nasafi says these claims lead to 'incapacity of the Creator' (عجز الباري) to distinguish truth from falsehood. Ask yourself: why would that be a necessary consequence?
3
Recall the established truth about God's power
Nasafi then states that clear proofs have established that God is perfect in power and that incapacity is impossible for Him. Reflect on how this contradicts the implication from step 2.
4
Draw the conclusion
Understand the conclusion: because God is perfectly powerful and His will is effective, He must be able to distinguish the prophet from the pretender. This refutes the opponents' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما تعلق به الفرق الآخر من امتناع التفرقة بين النبي والمتنبئ لامتناع(۱) تغيير الطباع، أو لإمكان المعارضة وغير ذلك (۲)، كل ذلك موجب عجز الباري جل وعلا عن التفرقة بين الصادق والكاذب، وقد ثبت بالدلائل الواضحة أن صانع العالم جل وعلا كامل القدرة يستحيل عليه العجز فكان قادرًا على ذلك ضرورة كمال قدرته ونفاذ مشيئته.
And what the other faction clung to—of the impossibility of distinguishing between the prophet and the pretender due to the impossibility of changing natures, or the possibility of opposition and other things—all that necessitates the incapacity of the Creator, may He be exalted and glorified, to distinguish between the truthful and the liar. And it has been established by clear proofs that the Creator of the world, may He be exalted and glorified, is perfect in power, incapacity is impossible for Him, so He is able to do that necessarily due to the perfection of His power and the execution of His will.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage refutes those who deny the possibility of distinguishing a true prophet from a false pretender (mutanabbi) on grounds such as the impossibility of changing natural laws (طباع) or the possibility of counterfeiting miracles (معارضة). Nasafi argues that such objections imply that God is incapable of distinguishing truth from falsehood—which contradicts clear proofs that God is perfectly powerful (كامل القدرة) and incapable of weakness. Therefore, He must be able to distinguish, by necessity of His perfect power and effective will.

The Possibility of Prophethood and the Need for Rational Moral Judgment
Nasafi concludes that sending messengers is possible and not foolish, and introduces the need for rational basis of moral judgments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Nasafi concludes prophethood is rationally possible and that moral concepts (good/evil) require rational proof.

Core Idea
Prophethood is rationally possible; moral knowledge must be rationally based.

In the previous discussions, Nasafi laid arguments that prophethood is not inherently impossible. Now he draws a clear conclusion: sending messengers is a possibility, and every possibility must be proven rationally. He then sets the stage for the next topic: how do we know good and evil without revelation? This lesson captures that pivotal transition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise style and the clear conclusion.
The passage is short but dense; it concludes the previous discussion and introduces the next.
2
Identify the three key claims
Find where Nasafi says (a) sending messengers is not impossible, (b) it is not foolish, (c) it is in the realm of possibles. Understand what 'possible' means in kalam: neither necessary nor impossible.
The word 'mumkinat' (possibles) is a technical term.
3
Understand the transition to rational moral knowledge
The last part says 'we will now speak on... there is no knowledge of wisdom and foolishness, and good and evil except...' (cut off). This sets up a discussion on how we know right and wrong if revelation is not yet considered. Nasafi implies we need rational proof for moral judgments.
This is a key debate: can reason alone determine good and evil? This passage foreshadows that discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وظهر بهذا أن إرسال الرسل ليس بمستحيل في نفسه ولا سفه في ذاته في حيز الممكنات، وكل ممكن يثبت عند قيام الدليل، وقد قام على ما نبين على هذه الطريقة فنتكلم الآن في أن لا وقوف على الحكمة والسفه والحسن والقبح إلا

Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This short passage serves as a conclusion and transition. Nasafi states that from the preceding arguments it is clear that sending messengers (prophets) is not impossible (mustahil) nor foolish (sufah); it falls within the realm of possibilities (mumkinat). Since it is possible, it can be proven rationally. He then announces that the next topic will be about the rational basis for knowing wisdom and foolishness, and good and evil (ḥusn wa qubḥ)—a key Mu'tazili-Ash'ari debating point. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continued on the next page.

God's Actions Are Not Foolish: The Argument from Authority
Imam Nasafi defines foolishness and shows it cannot apply to God's actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of foolishness in Islamic theology and why it cannot apply to God's actions.

Core Idea
Foolishness is defined by violating a prohibition, and no one can prohibit God.

Imam Nasafi addresses a challenge to prophethood: if sending messengers seems useless, is God foolish? He clarifies that foolishness depends on authority. Since no one has authority over God, His actions cannot be foolish. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is a refutation of an objection about prophethood.
2
Understand the definition of foolishness
Note that the passage defines foolishness not by lack of benefit but by violation of a prohibition from an authority. This is key to the argument.
In the witnessed world (al-shahid), an action is foolish only if it is prohibited.
3
Apply the definition to God
Since God has no authority over Him, no prohibition can apply. Therefore, His actions cannot be foolish.
This step refutes the claim that sending messengers is foolish.
4
See the examples of human foolishness
The examples show actions become foolish because the Creator forbade them. This cannot be transferred to the unseen (al-gha'ib).
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that God's actions are not foolish, so the objection is unsound. Consider how this defends prophethood.
This lesson establishes a foundational principle for kalam arguments about God's actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سفها ما لم يكن منهيًا عنه من قبل من له ولاية الأمر والنهي، وكل تصرف أو فعل في الشاهد ما كان سفهًا إلا لكونه منهيًا عنه، وما كان فاعله سفيها إلا لارتكابه نهي خالقه، ولا نهي لأحد على الله تعالى لما يستحيل دخوله تحت نهي غيره، إذ لا ولاية لغيره عليه ولا قدرة، فلا يتصور كون فعله سفها، فكل من أمر غيره بما لا منفعة له فيه أو نهى عما لا مضرة له فيه أو أرسل رسولاً إلى من يكذب رسوله أو يقصده بمكروه أو فعل فعلاً لا منفعة له فيه كان سفيهًا، لأن صانعه نهاه عن هذه الأفعال فكان سبب كون الفعل سفهًا هذا هو، ويستحيل تعديته إلى الغائب فلم يكن فعلاً ما من الصانع سفها فلم يستقم منكم هذا الكلام.
Foolishness is not [something] unless it is prohibited by one who has authority over command and prohibition. And every action or deed in the witnessed world was not foolish except because it was prohibited, and its doer was not foolish except for committing the prohibition of his Creator. There is no prohibition for anyone over Allah the Exalted because it is impossible for Him to be under the prohibition of another, since there is no authority for another over Him nor power. So it is inconceivable that His action be foolish. For whoever commands another with something that has no benefit in it, or prohibits something that has no harm in it, or sends a messenger to one who will deny his messenger or intends him with something disliked, or does an action that has no benefit in it, he is foolish, because his Maker prohibited him from these actions. So the cause of an action's being foolish is this, and it is impossible to transfer it to the Absent. So none of His actions are foolish. Therefore this speech of yours is not sound.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that sending messengers is foolish. Imam Nasafi first defines the basis of foolishness: an action is only foolish if it has been prohibited by someone in authority. In the witnessed world (al-shahid), an action is not considered foolish unless it violates a prohibition. Similarly, a person is not called foolish unless he violates his Creator's command. Then he applies this to God: there is no one who can prohibit God, since no one has authority or power over Him. Therefore, it is impossible for God's action to be foolish. He then gives examples of actions that would be foolish for a human because their Maker prohibited them—such as commanding useless things, prohibiting harmless things, or sending a messenger to someone who will deny him—and notes that this cause cannot be transferred to the unseen (al-gha'ib). Thus, the argument that God sending messengers is foolish is invalid.

Key Terms
سفه
ولاية الأمر والنهي
الشاهد
الغائب
صانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Foolishness is based on objective harm or benefit.
Correction: The passage clarifies that foolishness is defined by violation of a prohibition from an authority, not by lack of benefit or presence of harm.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes an action foolish?
A) Lack of benefit
B) Prohibition by an authority
C) Harm to others
D) Being unnecessary

Key Takeaway
An action is foolish only when it violates a prohibition; since no one can prohibit God, His actions are never foolish.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or see something in the world that seems pointless or harmful, do you ever doubt that God's action is wise? What concrete step can you take today to silence the inner critic that questions His decisions?

God's Power to Distinguish Prophet from False Prophet
Imam Nasafi refutes the objection that God cannot distinguish prophet from false prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi refutes the objection that God cannot distinguish a true prophet from a false prophet, affirming God's perfect power and will.

Core Idea
God's perfect power makes all distinctions possible, even between prophet and false prophet.

Some theologians argued that distinguishing a prophet from a false prophet is impossible — either because natures cannot be changed or because a false prophet could always oppose. Imam Nasafi shows that such an argument actually implies God's inability, which is impossible given His perfect power and will. This passage is a concise refutation grounded in divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
The objectors claim it's impossible to distinguish a prophet from a false prophet. Note the two reasons they give: impossibility of changing natures, and the possibility of opposition.
The objection initially seems about the nature of miracles or human claims.
2
See the Consequence
Imam Nasafi points out that this objection necessarily implies that God is unable to distinguish truth from falsehood. Understand why that is a serious problem.
The argument leads to attributing inability (ʿajz) to Allah.
3
Understand the Refutation
The refutation is based on clear proofs that God is perfect in power (kāmil al-qudra) and inability is impossible for Him. Therefore, He must be capable of making the distinction.
God's power and will are effective and unrestricted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما تعلق به الفرق الآخر من امتناع التفرقة بين النبي والمتنبئ لامتناع تغيير الطباع، أو لإمكان المعارضة وغير ذلك، كل ذلك موجب عجز الباري جل وعلا عن التفرقة بين الصادق والكاذب، وقد ثبت بالدلائل الواضحة أن صانع العالم جل وعلا كامل القدرة يستحيل عليه العجز فكان قادرًا على ذلك ضرورة كمال قدرته ونفاذ مشيئته.
And what the other group adhered to regarding the impossibility of distinguishing between a prophet and a false prophet, due to the impossibility of changing natures, or the possibility of opposition and other things, all of that necessitates the inability of the Creator, may He be glorified and exalted, to distinguish between the truthful and the liar. And it has been established by clear proofs that the Maker of the world, may He be glorified and exalted, is perfect in power, inability being impossible for Him. So He was capable of that of necessity due to the perfection of His power and the effectiveness of His will.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the distinction between a true prophet (nabi) and a false prophet (mutanabbi'). The objectors claim that it is impossible to distinguish between them because natures cannot be changed, or because the false prophet could produce similar claims. Imam Nasafi argues that such objections lead to the conclusion that God is unable (ʿājiz) to distinguish the truthful from the liar, which contradicts God's perfect power (kāmil al-qudra). Since it is proven that God is omnipotent and inability is impossible for Him, He must be capable of making such a distinction. The argument shows that any claim implying God's inability is false and that God's power and will are effective to make the distinction possible.

Misconception: The objection is about the nature of miracles or the criteria for prophethood.
Correction: The passage shows the real issue is whether God has the power to distinguish between truth and falsehood. The objection implicitly denies God's perfect power, which is refuted by affirming His omnipotence.

Checkpoint

B) God's inability

Key Takeaway

Prophethood Is Possible; Good and Evil Known Only by Revelation
Nasafi concludes prophethood is possible and asserts revelation is necessary for moral knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Nasafi affirms that sending messengers is rationally possible and that moral values are known only through revelation, not by intellect alone.

Core Idea
Prophethood is possible; morality requires revelation.

Imam Nasafi has concluded that sending messengers is neither impossible nor foolish. He now transitions to a crucial epistemological principle: intellect alone cannot determine good and evil. This lesson unpacks that conclusion and the shift to revelation as the sole source of moral knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is one long sentence containing two main claims.
2
Identify the two claims
First, Nasafi asserts that sending messengers is possible (not impossible or foolish). Second, he states that wisdom, folly, good, and evil are known only through revelation.
These two claims are distinct but connected: the first is about the possibility of prophethood, the second about how we know moral values.
3
Understand the 'possible' category
Nasafi says every possible thing is established when proof exists. Note that he does not say prophethood is necessary; rather, it is possible and requires proof.
This shows the rational category of 'possible' (mumkin) is central to kalam arguments.
4
Grasp the role of revelation
The passage explicitly says there is no knowledge of moral values except through revelation (al-sam‘). Intellects are not instruments (āla) for this knowledge.
This is a key Ash‘ari / Maturidi position: ethics are known from Divine revelation, not from reason alone.
5
Note the exception
Nasafi mentions that all Ahl al-Hadith theologians except Abu al-‘Abbas al-Qalanisi hold this view. Understand that there was a dissenting opinion.
Al-Qalanisi held that some moral values could be known by intellect, but the majority disagreed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وظهر بهذا أن إرسال الرسل ليس بمستحيل في نفسه ولا سفه في ذاته في حيز الممكنات، وكل ممكن يثبت عند قيام الدليل، وقد قام على ما نبين على هذه الطريقة فنتكلم الآن في أن لا وقوف على الحكمة والسفه والحسن والقبح إلا بالسمع وتخرج العقول من أن تكون آله للاطلاع على ما ذهب إليه جميع متكلمي أهل الحديث سوى أبي العباس القلانسي.
And it became clear by this that sending messengers is not impossible in itself, nor foolish in its essence, within the realm of possibilities. And every possible thing is established when proof exists, and proof has been established according to what we will explain in this method. So now we will speak about that there is no knowledge of wisdom and folly and good and evil except through revelation, and that intellects are excluded from being instruments for attaining that, which is the opinion of all theologians of Ahl al-Hadith except Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion and transition. It first concludes that sending messengers (irsāl al-rusul) is not impossible (mustaḥīl) in itself, nor foolish (safah) in its essence; it falls within the realm of possibilities (ḥayyiz al-mumkināt). It then notes that any possible thing is established when a proof (dalīl) exists, and proof has been given according to the method to be explained. Next, it transitions to a new topic: the epistemological question of how we know moral values—wisdom (ḥikma), folly (safah), good (ḥusn), and evil (qubḥ). Imam Nasafi affirms that such knowledge is only attainable through revelation (al-samʿ), not through pure intellect; intellects are not instruments (āla) for this. He notes that this is the position of all Ahl al-Hadith theologians except Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi, who held a different view.

Key Terms
مستحيل
سفه
ممكن
دليل
سمع
عقل

الحسن
القبح
آلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intellect alone can determine what is good and evil.
Correction: The passage states that wisdom, folly, good, and evil are known only through revelation, and intellects are not instruments for that knowledge.

Checkpoint

B) By revelation only
C) By intuition
D) By consensus

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is rationally possible, but moral knowledge comes solely from revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When did you last face a moral dilemma and instinctively relied on your own reasoning before consulting the Qur'an and Sunnah? What might that reveal about your trust in revelation as the sole source of moral knowledge?
?
The passage asserts that sending messengers is not foolish. Do you treat the messengers' guidance as essential wisdom for your life, or do you sometimes see it as optional?
?
If you truly believe that only revelation can teach you good and evil, what concrete step can you take today to prioritize learning the revealed rulings over your own opinions?

Disagreement on the Ruling of Prophethood
Imam Nasafi presents the dispute: Ahl al-Hadith say prophethood is possible; rationalists say it is necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether prophethood is possible or necessary, and what the Mu'tazila mean by 'obligatory' prophethood.

Core Idea
Prophethood's modal status divides theologians: possible vs necessary, but 'necessary' means determined by wisdom, not imposed duty.

Imam Nasafi presents a key dispute among Islamic theologians: Is prophethood possible in the intellect, or is it necessary? The Ahl al-Hadith hold one view, while the rationalists (Mu'tazila) hold another. But their concept of 'necessity' is subtle—they do not mean God is under obligation, but that prophethood is guaranteed to occur because of God's wisdom. This passage clarifies that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two views
The passage begins by asking if prophethood is possible or necessary. Identify which group says it is possible, and which says it is necessary.
The first group is the majority of Ahl al-Hadith; the second includes rationalists who affirm the obligation of thanking the Benefactor.
2
Understand the key terms: mumkin and wājib
Note that 'mumkin' means rationally possible, and 'wājib' usually means obligatory. But the Mu'tazila redefine 'wājib' here.
They say 'wujūb' is an expression for the strength of existence, not a duty imposed on God.
3
Grasp the Mu'tazila's reinterpretation of wājib
They equate 'obligatory' with 'determined to exist' (muta'akkid al-wujūd) because prophethood is a requirement of God's wisdom.
They argue that if God's wisdom requires something, its non-existence would be foolishness (safah), impossible for God.
4
Reflect on the analogy with God's knowledge
Just as what God knows will exist must come to be, so too prophethood must exist because it is a requirement of His wisdom.
This shows that necessity here is about inevitability, not obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هل الرسالة من قبل الممكنات في العقل أم من جملة الواجبات: ثم إن متكلمي أهل الإسلام اختلفوا فيما بينهم أن الرسالة من قبل الممكنات في العقل، أم هي من جملة الواجبات؟ فذهب جميع متكلمي أهل الحديث سوى أبي العباس القلانسي إلى أنها من جملة الممكنات، وذهب القائلون بأن العقل آلة معرفة الحسن والقبيح ووجوب شكر المنعم إلى القول بوجوبها، ولا يعنون بكونها واجبة أنها وجبت على الله بإيجاب أحد أو بإيجابه على نفسه بل يريدون أنها متحققة الوجود، ويقولون: إن الوجوب في الحقيقة لفظة يعبر بها عن تأكد وجود المذكور، كما أن الامتناع لفظة يعبر بها عن تأكد لا وجوده، وهما في الحقيقة متقابلان تقابل الأضداد، وإنما يقولون: إنها متأكدة الوجود لأنها من مقتضيات حكمة القديم جل وعلا، ويستحيل أن لا يوجد ما كان وجوده من مقتضيات حكمته لما أن انعدامه يكون من باب السفه وهو يستحيل على القديم عز وجل، هذا كما أن ما علم الله وجوده يتحقق وجوده لا محالة ويكون واجب الوجود أي متأكد الوجود، لا أن وجوبه بإيجاب أحد على الله تعالى بل لأن وجوده يتحقق لا محالة لما أن انعدامه يوجب الجهل به، وهو مما يستحيل على القديم عز وجل وإلى هذا القول ذهب جميع من يقول بوجوب شكر المنعم.
[Question: Is prophethood among the possible things in the intellect, or among the necessary things?] Then the theologians of Islam differed among themselves: is prophethood among the possible things in the intellect, or among the necessary things? So all theologians of Ahl al-Hadith except Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi held that it is from the possible things. And those who hold that the intellect is an instrument for knowing good and evil and the obligation of thanking the Benefactor held that it is obligatory. But they do not mean by its being obligatory that it became obligatory upon God due to someone's imposition or due to His imposing it upon Himself. Rather, they mean that it is determined to exist. And they say: 'Obligation' in reality is an expression for the strength of the existence of the mentioned thing, just as 'impossibility' is an expression for the strength of its non-existence. And the two are in reality opposites. They say it is 'determined in existence' because it is from the requirements of the wisdom of the Eternal, may He be glorified and exalted, and it is impossible that what is from the requirements of His wisdom should not exist, because its non-existence would be from the category of foolishness, and that is impossible for the Eternal. This is just as what God knows will exist, its existence necessarily comes to be, and it is 'necessary of existence' i.e., 'determined in existence', not that its necessity is due to someone imposing upon God, but because its existence necessarily comes to be, since its non-existence would engender ignorance of it, and that is impossible for the Eternal. And this opinion was held by all who affirm the obligation of thanking the Benefactor.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents the theological dispute about the modal status of prophethood (risāla). It begins with the question: is prophethood among the possible things (al-mumkināt) in the intellect, or among the necessary things (al-wājibāt)? The first group, Ahl al-Hadith (except Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi), say it is possible. The second group, those who affirm rational knowledge of good and evil and the obligation to thank the Benefactor (shukr al-munʿim)—i.e., the Muʿtazila—say it is obligatory (wājiba). The passage then explains that by 'obligatory' they do not mean that anyone imposed it on God or that God imposed it on Himself; rather, they mean it is 'verified in existence' (mutaḥaqqiqat al-wujūd) or 'determined in existence' (mutaʾakkidat al-wujūd). They interpret 'obligation' (wujūb) as an expression for the strength of existence, just as 'impossibility' (imtināʿ) is an expression for the strength of non-existence. They consider prophethood to be determined in existence because it is a requirement of God's wisdom (ḥikma). Since God is wise, whatever His wisdom requires must exist; otherwise, its non-existence would be foolishness (safah), which is impossible for God. This is analogous to what God knows will exist: it necessarily comes to be, not because someone obligates God, but because non-existence would conflict with God's knowledge. Thus, for them, prophethood is necessary in the sense of being inevitable, not because of external obligation. The passage concludes by stating that all who affirm the obligation of thanking the Benefactor hold this view.

ممكن
واجب
وجوب
امتناع
حكمة

شكر المنعم
متأكد الوجود

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'obligatory' for prophethood means God is under an external or self-imposed duty.
Correction: The Mu'tazila clarify that it means 'determined to exist' as a necessary consequence of God's wisdom, not because anyone obligates Him.

Checkpoint
When the Mu'tazila say prophethood is obligatory, what do they actually mean?
A) God is forced by someone to send prophets
B) It is rationally impossible not to send prophets
C) It is determined to exist due to God's wisdom
D) It is a duty upon humans to accept

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila consider prophethood 'obligatory' not because anyone obligates God, but because it is a necessary consequence of His wisdom, just as what He knows must exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If prophethood is a necessary result of God's wisdom, why do you often treat the Prophet's message as optional in your daily choices? What would change if you saw it as essential as the sun rising?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that God's wisdom demands guidance; otherwise nonsending would be foolishness. Does your heart truly believe that God's wisdom is perfectly manifested in sending prophets? Or do you sometimes feel that the world would be fine without revelation?
?
When you hear 'obligation' in religion, do you first feel burdened or do you see it as a beautiful consequence of God's perfect attributes? How can you train your soul to embrace divine obligations as gifts from His wisdom, not impositions?

Reason's Limitation and the Need for Divine Guidance
Nasafi argues for prophethood by showing reason's inability to discern benefit from harm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi argues for the necessity of prophethood by demonstrating that human reason and senses alone cannot reliably distinguish between beneficial and harmful substances, thus requiring divine guidance.

Core Idea
Reason alone cannot distinguish food from poison; revelation is essential for survival.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses those who deny prophethood but accept that reason can know good and evil. He presents a practical scenario: God created foods and poisons, but human reason and senses cannot tell them apart without dangerous experimentation. This leads to a proof that without divine clarification, creation would be futile. The passage sets the stage for a two-part argument, though the second part is cut off in our source.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Understand the setting: Nasafi argues on the deniers' own ground (reason can know good/evil). Notice the scenario of food and poison.
The passage includes a long hypothetical example.
2
Identify the central problem
Reason and senses cannot distinguish poisons from foods; experimentation is risky and delayed effects make it impossible.
Not all poisons kill instantly, and some effects appear after months.
3
Examine the first aspect of the proof
If God did not clarify which substances are beneficial and which harmful, then creating foods would be futile (عبث). This implies the need for prophetic guidance.
The second aspect is incomplete in the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الممتنعات فعل ذلك بالعقل دون السمع، إذ السمع بلا ثبوت الرسالة محال، فثبت من نفاة الرسالة المساعدة على القول بأن العقل مما يوقف به على الحكمة والسفه والواجب والممتنع والحسن والقبيح. فنتكلم معهم على هذه الطريقة بناء للأمر على مساعدتهم دون الاشتغال بإثبات معرفة ذلك بمجرد العقول بالدليل فنقول وبالله التوفيق إن من المعلوم الذي لا خفاء به على من اعترف بالصانع أن الصانع جل جلالة خلق الخلق ولم يجعل على ما التامة ويزيل عنهم العلل العارضة من الأدوية، خلق من جنس تلك الجواهر السموم القاتلة والجواهر المتلفة، وليس في قوة الحواس ولا في جبلة العقل الوقوف على ما يمتاز به البعض عن البعض ولا الاطلاع على ما يوجب التفرقة بين كل من ذلك، ولا يطبق العقل التجربة بنفسه لما فيه من خطر الهلاك ولا بمن تحت يديه لما فيه من تعريض الغير على التو أو التلف، مع أن ما هو المقصود من الوقوف على ذلك لا يحصل إلا بالتجربة إذ ليس ولو أمر غيره ممن تحت تصرفه بتناول (ذلك يتناول) هو بنفسه ذلك ليدفع به عن نفسه ألم الجوع وضرر العطش ويحصل له به ما هو محبوبة من البقاء إذ لم ير أثر ضرر ظهر عن تناوله لأدى ذلك عسى إلى تلف مهجته وفساد بنيته بعد ذلك بزمان لما كان ذلك المتناول مما يضر بعد شهر أو سنة، ولو أمسك عن تناول الأغذية منتظرا ظهور أثره إلى شهر أو سنة لمات جوعًا وعطشاً قبل بلوغ ما ينتظره من المدة. ثم بعد هذا نقرر الكلام من وجوه أحدها أن الصانع جل وعلا لو لم يبين الأغذية من الأدوية والسموم، ولم يتجاسر العقلاء على التجربة، ولم يتوصلوا إلى الانتفاع بالأغذية، واستدامة مهجتهم، لم يكن لخلق الأغذية التي هي ما عليه العادة الجارية أسباب البقاء حكمة ويحصل إيجادها على العبث والسفه جل الله عن ذلك. والثاني: أن أبدان الممتحنين لو لم يجعل لهم الوصول إلى ما هو من أسباب
There is no meaning in engaging in explaining the proofs of this matter, and I have already explained that exhaustively in a work of mine on that matter. Rather, the need here is to state the necessity of it for those who deny it. There is no doubt that whoever denies prophethood and considers it among the impossibilities does so by reason, not by revelation, for revelation without the establishment of prophethood is impossible. So it is established from the deniers of prophethood agreement on the statement that reason is that by which one knows wisdom and foolishness, the obligatory and the impossible, and good and evil. So we speak with them on this method, building on their agreement, without engaging in proving that knowledge by reason alone through evidence. We say, with God's success: It is known, with no hiddenness to one who acknowledges the Creator, that the Creator, may His majesty be exalted, created creation and did not make their survival customary except through foods, and He made them love survival, and He made in the kind of what He created what the creation desire by their natures due to pleasure and appetite. Then, just as He created foods for their survival, and medicines to preserve their perfect health and remove accidental ailments, He created from the same kind of substances deadly poisons and harmful substances. There is not in the power of the senses nor in the innate nature of the intellect the ability to know what distinguishes some from others, nor to discover what necessitates differentiation between each of that. The intellect cannot undertake experimentation by itself due to the danger of destruction, nor by those under its authority due to exposing others to loss or destruction, even though what is intended from knowing that is not obtained except through experimentation, since not every poison kills instantly, nor does every harm show its effect immediately after ingestion. For among poisons are those whose effect appears and whose harm intensifies after a week, and among them are those whose effect appears after a month and after a year – that is known among specialists in the science of the substances of things and the natures of existents. If one commanded another under his authority to ingest something while he himself ingested it to repel the pain of hunger and the harm of thirst and to obtain the beloved survival, since he did not see a harmful effect from its ingestion, perhaps that would lead to the destruction of his life and the corruption of his constitution after a time, because that ingested substance harms after a month or a year. And if he refrained from eating foods, waiting for its effect to appear for a month or a year, he would die of hunger and thirst before reaching the period he was waiting for. Then after this we establish the discussion from aspects. First: If the Creator, Exalted and High, did not distinguish foods from medicines and poisons, and the rational did not dare to experiment, and they did not arrive at benefiting from foods and maintaining their lives, there would be no wisdom in creating foods, which are the customary causes of survival, and their creation would be futile and foolish – God is exalted above that. Second: That the bodies of those tested, if they were not given access to what is from the causes...
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a preface and partial proof for the necessity of prophethood. The author begins by stating that he has already treated the proofs elsewhere; his present aim is to argue with those who deny prophethood on their own terms, accepting their assumption that reason can know good and evil. He then sets up a scenario: God created foods for survival and poisons that destroy. Reason and senses cannot distinguish between them without experimentation, but experimentation is dangerous and may lead to death or harm. Furthermore, the effects of some substances are delayed, so simple trial is unreliable. If a person were to test a substance on himself, he might die later; if he waited to see effects, he would starve. Thus, human reason alone is insufficient to secure survival. From this, the author begins a two-aspect proof. The first aspect: if God did not clarify which substances are beneficial and which harmful, then creating foods would be futile, contradicting divine wisdom. The second aspect is cut off at the page end, but it likely continues to argue that without revelation, there would be no access to the causes of survival, again implying futility. The passage is a kalam argument using a hypothetical scenario to demonstrate the need for prophetic guidance.

الأغذية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that reason alone can fully determine benefit and harm in all things.
Correction: The author shows the practical impossibility due to lack of experience and delayed effects, making reason insufficient.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is experimentation alone insufficient to distinguish between foods and poisons?
A) Because all poisons kill instantly.
B) Because experimentation requires revelation to be valid.
C) Because the effects of some substances are delayed, and experimenting oneself or others carries fatal risks.
D) Because reason and senses can distinguish without experimentation.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that without revelation, humans could not reliably distinguish between what sustains and what destroys life, making prophethood rationally necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Are you living with the awareness that every detail of your life depends on Allah's guidance, or do you often depend on your own judgment? What would change if you truly internalized that reason alone is insufficient?

The Problem of Empirical Experimentation
Al-Nasafi explains the practical impossibility of safe empirical experimentation on foods and poisons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why unaided human reason and personal experimentation cannot reliably distinguish between nourishing and harmful substances.

Core Idea
Delayed effects make personal experimentation dangerous and unreliable.

Before presenting his three arguments for the necessity of divine revelation, Imam al-Nasafi establishes a crucial premise: humans cannot safely determine which substances are beneficial or harmful through personal trial. Many poisons show their deadly effects only after weeks or months, making direct experimentation deadly and indirect experimentation unethical. This sets the stage for why revelation is indispensable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Nasafi uses a very reasoned empirical argument.
2
Identify the dilemma
The passage presents a practical dilemma: waiting to see effects leads to starvation, while consuming without knowledge risks delayed harm.
This dilemma shows the impossibility of safe personal testing.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this inability to test safely makes divine guidance necessary. What would happen if we had no revelation?
This connects to the three arguments that follow.

العالم بجواهرها وما يتعلق بها من الحكمة على لسان ما شاء من عباده، ليخرج تخليق الجواهر التي هي من أسباب البقاء وأحداث الأبدان المحتاجة في دوامها وبقائها إلى الأغذية المجعولة أسبابًا لذلك عن السفه ويدخل في حيز ما هو حكمة. والثالث: أن بعد وقوفهم على ما يتعلق به بقاء أبدانهم، ولا شك أن كلا جبلوا على حب البقاء وطلب ما يحصل لهم به الدوام، فلو لم يشرع لهم الحكيم شرعًا يؤمرون بالتمسك به والانتهاء إلى حدوده مشتملاً على أسباب تبنى عليها الأحكام ويعرف بها اختصاص كل من اختص بشيء منها بما هو حكمه. وينقطع طمع من لاحظ له من تلك الأسباب عن الفوز بتلك الأحكام لتسارع كل إلى ما عرف به بقاءه ولتناول ما يرجو فيه دوامه، فيقع حينئذ بينهم أقبح تجاذب وأقطع تغالب، ثم يفضي ذلك إلى التقاتل، وذلك مما تؤول عاقبته إلى التفاني وانقطاع النسل وارتفاع الجنس وانعدام عاقبة لإيجاد الممتحنين سوى الفناء خاصة وذلك سفه. وكذا لا شك أن في أجزاء العالم ما كانت الطبائع إليه مائلة والرغبات فيه ممتدة والنفوس إلى الاستيلاء عليه والاختصاص بالاستمتاع به متسارعة، والقلوب بنبله والظفر به والوصول إلى الارتفاق به منشرحة مرتاحة من نحو الجواري
regarding how some differ from others, and not knowing what necessitates differentiation between each of that; and the intellect cannot undertake experimentation itself because of the risk of destruction, nor with those under its authority because it would expose others to death or loss. Yet the intended goal of ascertaining that is not attained except through experimentation, since not every poison kills instantly, nor does every harmful thing show its effect immediately after consumption. Among poisons are those whose effect appears and whose harm intensifies after a week, and among them are those whose effect appears after a month or a year—well-known to specialists in the science of the essences of things and the natures of existents. And if one orders another under his authority to consume that, then he himself consumes it to ward off from himself the pain of hunger and the harm of thirst and to obtain what is desired of survival, since he saw no harmful effect from its consumption, that would perhaps lead to the destruction of his soul and the corruption of his constitution after some time, because that consumed substance harms after a month or a year. And if he refrains from consuming nourishments, waiting for their effect to appear for a month or a year, he would die of hunger and thirst before reaching the time he awaits. Then after this we present the discussion in aspects: One of them is that the Maker, may He be exalted and glorified, if He had not distinguished nourishments from medicines and poisons, and the rational people had not dared to experiment and had not arrived at benefiting from nourishments and preserving their lives, then the creation of nourishments, which are according to the customary means of survival, would have no wisdom, and their existence would be vain and foolish—exalted is Allah above that. The second is that if the bodies of those being tested were not given access to what are means of their survival, they would perish in the shortest time and swiftest period without achieving any purpose for their existence other than annihilation; and creating creatures solely for annihilation, with no praiseworthy consequence attached to it, is considered repugnant by intellects and outside of wisdom. Therefore, a declaration must come from the Originator of things upon their diverse natures, the Knower of their essences and the wisdom pertaining to them, upon the tongue of whomever He wills among His servants, so that the creation of essences that are means of survival and the generation of bodies that need for their continuity and survival the nourishments made as means for that may be removed from folly and enter into the realm of wisdom. The third is that after they come to know what pertains to the survival of their bodies—and there is no doubt that everyone is naturally predisposed to love of survival and seeking what obtains continuity for them—if the Wise One had not legislated for them a law commanding them to adhere to it and to stop at its limits, comprising causes upon which rulings are based and by which the specific entitlement of each one who is entitled to something is known as its due, and the hope of one who has no share from those causes of attaining those rulings is cut off, then each would hasten toward what he knows ensures his survival and would consume what he hopes will give him continuity. Then a most ugly tug-of-war and a most severe struggle would occur among them, leading to fighting, and that would result in mutual annihilation, cessation of progeny, extinction of the species, and the absence of any outcome for the creation of those being tested other than annihilation alone—and that is foolish. Likewise, there is no doubt that among the parts of the world are those toward which natures incline, desires extend, souls hasten to possess and monopolize enjoyment, and hearts are delighted and comforted by procuring them, obtaining them, and reaching benefit from them, such as slave girls...
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents the author's argument for the necessity of divine revelation (shar') to guide humanity in distinguishing beneficial foods from harmful substances, followed by three distinct proofs (wujuh) that establish the wisdom behind such guidance. The author begins by describing the practical impossibility of relying on unaided human reason and personal experimentation. Not all harmful effects appear immediately—some poisons kill after weeks or months—so a person cannot safely determine what is nourishing and what is poisonous through personal trial. Attempting to experiment on others is also impermissible because it endangers them. Thus, empirical knowledge about substances is necessary but inaccessible through safe experimentation. The author then states that after clarifying this problem, he will present the discussion in several aspects. The first aspect is that if the Creator (al-Ṣāniʿ) had not clearly distinguished foods from medicines and poisons, rational people would not dare to experiment, and they would not be able to benefit from food. Then the creation of food as a means of survival would have no wisdom; it would be vain and foolish (safah), and Allah is exalted above that. The second aspect is that if bodies were not provided with means of survival, they would perish almost immediately, making the creation for annihilation alone senseless and lacking a praiseworthy end. Therefore, a declaration (bayān) from the Creator, who knows the essences of things, is necessary—delivered through a chosen messenger—so that the creation of essences that sustain life and the formation of bodies dependent on food may be removed from folly and placed within the realm of wisdom. The third aspect is that once people know what ensures their survival—and they are naturally inclined to love survival—if no divine law (sharʿ) were legislated commanding adherence to its limits and specifying rights, then everyone would grab what they think ensures survival, leading to conflict, fighting, mutual annihilation, and extinction of offspring. That would be sheer folly. The author adds that there are objects of desire (e.g., slave girls) that people naturally compete for, which would exacerbate the conflict. Thus, divine revelation is absolutely necessary to prevent destruction and to give wisdom to creation.

Key Terms
التجربة (al-tajriba, empirical experimentation)
السموم (al-sumūm, poisons)
الهلاك (al-halāk, destruction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think empiricism is always reliable for individuals
Correction: The passage shows that delayed effects make personal experimentation unsafe; only specialists with accumulated knowledge can assess substances, but even they need revelation for definitive distinction.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it dangerous for a person to rely solely on personal experimentation to determine if a food is safe?
A) Because all poisons kill instantly.
B) Because the effects of some harmful substances appear long after consumption.
C) Because human intellect is too weak to understand natural substances.
D) Because experimentation is forbidden by reason.

Key Takeaway
Human experimentation alone cannot guarantee safety because harmful effects may appear long after consumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a decision about what is truly beneficial for you—whether in food, relationships, or actions—do you trust your own immediate judgment, or do you seek guidance from the One who knows all outcomes? How does this passage challenge your reliance on your own experience?
?
If you cannot be sure that what you consume today will not harm you a year from now, how does that make you feel about your ability to secure your own well-being? Where do you turn for certainty?
?
This passage shows that even something as basic as eating requires divine guidance. Are there areas of your life where you still insist on 'experimenting' yourself rather than turning to Allah's revelation? What is one concrete step you can take today to rely more on His guidance?

First Argument: Wisdom Requires Revelation
Al-Nasafi's first argument for the necessity of revelation based on the wisdom of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's first argument: without revelation, the creation of food as a means of survival would lack wisdom and be futile.

Core Idea
Without revelation, food would be useless, making creation foolish.

Having established the impossibility of safe human experimentation, al-Nasafi presents his first formal argument. If the Creator had not clearly distinguished foods from poisons through revelation, rational people would never dare to experiment and would not benefit from food. The very existence of food as a means of survival would then be without wisdom, which is unworthy of the All-Wise Creator. Thus, revelation is necessary to give meaning to the creation of nourishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument in Arabic
Read the Arabic passage noting its conditional structure: 'if... not... then...'.
2
Identify the key terms
Find terms like الحكمة (wisdom), السفه (folly), and الصانع (the Maker). Understand their significance.
3
Understand the logical chain
Trace the conditional: If no revelation → no one dares to experiment → no benefit from food → food creation lacks wisdom → that would be foolish. Therefore, revelation is necessary to avoid foolishness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على ما يمتاز به البعض عن البعض ولا الاطلاع على ما يوجب التفرقة بين كل من ذلك، ولا يطبق العقل التجربة بنفسه لما فيه من خطر الهلاك ولا بمن تحت يديه لما فيه من تعريض الغير على التو أو التلف، مع أن ما هو المقصود من الوقوف على ذلك لا يحصل إلا بالتجربة إذ ليس كل سم يقتل من ساعته، ولا كل مضر يظهر أثره عقيب تناوله، إذ من السموم ما يظهر أثره ويتفاقم ضرره بعد أسبوع، ومنه ما يظهر ذلك بعد شهر وبعد سنة، معروف ذلك عند المختصين بعلم جواهر الأشياء وطبائع الموجودات، ولو أمر غيره ممن تحت تصرفه بتناول ذلك يتناول هو بنفسه ذلك ليدفع به عن نفسه ألم الجوع وضرر العطش ويحصل له به ما هو محبوبة من البقاء إذ لم ير أثر ضرر ظهر عن تناوله لأدى ذلك عسى إلى تلف مهجته وفساد بنيته بعد ذلك بزمان لما كان ذلك المتناول مما يضر بعد شهر أو سنة، ولو أمسك عن تناول الأغذية منتظرا ظهور أثره إلى شهر أو سنة لمات جوعاً وعطشاً قبل بلوغ ما ينتظره من المدة. ثم بعد هذا نقرر الكلام من وجوه أحدها أن الصانع جل وعلا لو لم يبين الأغذية من الأدوية والسموم، ولم يتجاسر العقلاء على التجربة، ولم يتوصلوا إلى الانتفاع بالأغذية، واستدامة مهجتهم، لم يكن لخلق الأغذية التي هي ما عليه العادة الجارية أسباب البقاء حكمة ويحصل إيجادها على العبث والسفه جل الله عن ذلك. والثاني: أن أبدان الممتحنين لو لم يجعل لهم الوصول إلى ما هو من أسباب بقائها تلفت بأوهى مدة وأسرع زمان من غير حصول معنى لإيجادهم سوى الفناء، وخلق الخلق للفناء خاصة من غير عاقبة حميدة تتعلق به مستقبح في العقول خارج عن الحكمة فلا بد من البيان الوارد من منشئ الأشياء على طبائعها المختلفة، العالم بجواهرها وما يتعلق بها من الحكمة على لسان ما شاء من عباده، ليخرج تخليق الجواهر التي هي من أسباب البقاء وأحداث الأبدان المحتاجة في دوامها وبقائها إلى الأغذية المجعولة أسبابًا لذلك عن السفه ويدخل في حيز ما هو حكمة. والثالث: أن بعد وقوفهم على ما يتعلق به بقاء أبدانهم، ولا شك أن كلا جبلوا على حب البقاء وطلب ما يحصل لهم به الدوام، فلو لم يشرع لهم الحكيم شرعًا يؤمرون بالتمسك به والانتهاء إلى حدوده مشتملاً على أسباب تبنى عليها الأحكام ويعرف بها اختصاص كل من اختص بشيء منها بما هو حكمه. وينقطع طمع من لاحظ له من تلك الأسباب عن الفوز بتلك الأحكام لتسارع كل إلى ما عرف به بقاءه ولتناول ما يرجو فيه How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If the creation of food would be foolish without revelation, then how much more foolish are our lives when we ignore the revelation that gives meaning and purpose to our existence? What would change in your daily routine if you truly believed that ignoring revelation makes your life vain?

Second Argument: Revelation Prevents Futile Annihilation
The second argument: revelation is needed to avoid creating for mere annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's second argument: without revelation, human existence would be solely for annihilation, which is repugnant to reason.

Core Idea
Without revelation, creation would be pointless destruction.

Al-Nasafi's second proof moves from the individual to the broader purpose of creation. If bodies are not given access to means of survival through revelation, they would quickly perish without achieving any meaningful purpose. Creating humans solely for annihilation, with no praiseworthy consequence, is irrational and unworthy of a wise Creator. Therefore, a divine declaration (bayān) is necessary to give meaning to creation and avoid folly.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic carefully and identify the conditional 'if... then...' structure.
2
Focus on the idea of 'praiseworthy consequence'
The text says that creating for annihilation alone is 'repugnant to intellects'. Understand why a purely transient existence without an eternal outcome is problematic.
3
See the solution
Al-Nasafi concludes that a bayān (revelation) from the Creator is necessary to lift creation out of folly and into wisdom.

And if he refrains from consuming nourishments, waiting for their effect to appear for a month or a year, he would die of hunger and thirst before reaching the time he awaits. Then after this we present the discussion in aspects: One of them is that the Maker, may He be exalted and glorified, if He had not distinguished nourishments from medicines and poisons, and the rational people had not dared to experiment and had not arrived at benefiting from nourishments and preserving their lives, then the creation of nourishments, which are according to the customary means of survival, would have no wisdom, and their existence would be vain and foolish—exalted is Allah above that. The second is that if the bodies of those being tested were not given access to what are means of their survival, they would perish in the shortest time and swiftest period without achieving any purpose for their existence other than annihilation; and creating creatures solely for annihilation, with no praiseworthy consequence attached to it, is considered repugnant by intellects and outside of wisdom. Therefore, a declaration must come from the Originator of things upon their diverse natures, the Knower of their essences and the wisdom pertaining to them, upon the tongue of whomever He wills among His servants, so that the creation of essences that are means of survival and the generation of bodies that need for their continuity and survival the nourishments made as means for that may be removed from folly and enter into the realm of wisdom. The third is that after they come to know what pertains to the survival of their bodies—and there is no doubt that everyone is naturally predisposed to love of survival and seeking what obtains continuity for them—if the Wise One had not legislated for them a law commanding them to adhere to it and to stop at its limits, comprising causes upon which rulings are based and by which the specific entitlement of each one who is entitled to something is known as its due, and the hope of one who has no share from those causes of attaining those rulings is cut off, then each would hasten toward what he knows ensures his survival and would consume what he hopes will give him continuity. Then a most ugly tug-of-war and a most severe struggle would occur among them, leading to fighting, and that would result in mutual annihilation, cessation of progeny, extinction of the species, and the absence of any outcome for the creation of those being tested other than annihilation alone—and that is foolish. Likewise, there is no doubt that among the parts of the world are those toward which natures incline, desires extend, souls hasten to possess and monopolize enjoyment, and hearts are delighted and comforted by procuring them, obtaining them, and reaching benefit from them, such as slave girls...
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents the author's argument for the necessity of divine revelation (shar') to guide humanity in distinguishing beneficial foods from harmful substances, followed by three distinct proofs (wujuh) that establish the wisdom behind such guidance. The author begins by describing the practical impossibility of relying on unaided human reason and personal experimentation. Not all harmful effects appear immediately—some poisons kill after weeks or months—so a person cannot safely determine what is nourishing and what is poisonous through personal trial. Attempting to experiment on others is also impermissible because it endangers them. Thus, empirical knowledge about substances is necessary but inaccessible through safe experimentation. The author then states that after clarifying this problem, he will present the discussion in several aspects. The first aspect is that if the Creator (al-Ṣāniʿ) had not clearly distinguished foods from medicines and poisons, rational people would not dare to experiment, and they would not be able to benefit from food. Then the creation of food as a means of survival would have no wisdom; it would be vain and foolish (safah), and Allah is exalted above that. The second aspect is that if bodies were not provided with means of survival, they would perish almost immediately, making the creation for annihilation alone senseless and lacking a praiseworthy end. Therefore, a declaration (bayān) from the Creator, who knows the essences of things, is necessary—delivered through a chosen messenger—so that the creation of essences that sustain life and the formation of bodies dependent on food may be removed from folly and placed within the realm of wisdom. The third aspect is that once people know what ensures their survival—and they are naturally inclined to love survival—if no divine law (sharʿ) were legislated commanding adherence to its limits and specifying rights, then everyone would grab what they think ensures survival, leading to conflict, fighting, mutual annihilation, and extinction of offspring. That would be sheer folly. The author adds that there are objects of desire (e.g., slave girls) that people naturally compete for, which would exacerbate the conflict. Thus, divine revelation is absolutely necessary to prevent destruction and to give wisdom to creation.

Key Terms
الفناء (al-fanāʾ, annihilation)
العاقبة الحميدة (al-ʿāqiba al-ḥamīda, praiseworthy outcome)
البيان (al-bayān, declaration/revelation)
الحكمة (al-ḥikma, wisdom)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, why is creating humans solely for annihilation considered repugnant?
A) Because humans love life.
B) Because it lacks a praiseworthy consequence and is outside wisdom.
C) Because annihilation is painful.
D) Because it would be a waste of resources.

Key Takeaway
Without revelation, human existence would be for annihilation alone, which contradicts divine wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your entire life were reduced to mere survival and eventual annihilation, how would you feel about your Creator? Does this lesson's argument that such a fate is 'repugnant' resonate with your own sense of worth? Will you thank Allah today for giving your life a greater purpose through revelation?
?
You have access to the means of survival—food, water, shelter. But are you living with a 'praiseworthy consequence' in mind? Or are you consuming life without seeking the wisdom that revelation offers? What specific change will you make this week to align your daily actions with a purpose beyond annihilation?
?
This argument says that without revelation, creation would be folly. Has the thought ever crossed your mind that life seems meaningless? How does knowing that revelation rescues creation from futility affect your trust in Allah's wisdom? Will you commit to studying revelation more deeply so that your life reflects its purpose?
